### Offshore Balancing Good -- Generic

#### Offshore balancing maintains world security without the risk of forward deployment. Rather than complete withdrawal from Europe and the Middle East, the US maintains promotes regional security agreements to prioritize developing a more secure Asia. 

**Walt 19** Stephen Walt is the Robert and Ren√©e Belfer Professor of
International Affairs at the Harvard Kennedy School. \["The End of
Hubris," *Foreign Affairs*, Vol. 98, Iss. 3, (May/Jun 2019): 26-35,
4-16-2019, URL:
<https://www.foreignaffairs.com/articles/2019-04-16/end-hubris>\] DTS

REALISM IN PRACTICE [Four presidents have now pursued a grand strategy
built around the goal of American hegemony, and all four have fared
poorly.]{.underline} As the political scientist John Mearsheimer and I
have argued previously in these pages, **[it is time for the United
States to return to its traditional approach of offshore
balancing.]{.underline}** This strategy begins by recognizing that [the
United States remains the most secure power]{.underline} in modern
history. [It has thousands of nuclear weapons and powerful conventional
forces]{.underline}, and it faces no serious rivals in the Western
Hemisphere. The Atlantic and Pacific Oceans still insulate the country
from many threats, giving U.S. leaders enormous latitude in choosing
where and when to fight. In addition to working to maintain U.S.
hegemony in the Western Hemisphere, American policymakers have long
sought to prevent other great powers from imitating the United States by
dominating their own regions. [A peer competitor with no serious rivals
nearby would be free to project power around the world-as Washington has
for decades]{.underline}. From an American perspective, **[it is better
if the major powers in Eurasia have to keep a wary eye on one another,
making it harder for them to interfere near American
shores]{.underline}**. [The United States intervened]{.underline} in the
world wars to prevent Wilhelmine Germany, Nazi Germany, and imperial
Japan from dominating Europe and Asia. [This same principle
inspired]{.underline} the Cold War strategy of
[containment,]{.underline} although in that case, the United States
could not pass the buck and had to bear most of the costs itself.
**[Today, there is no potential regional hegemon in Europe, whose states
should gradually take full responsibility for their own
defense.]{.underline}** The countries of [the European
Union]{.underline} are [home to more than 500 million people and boast a
combined annual gdp exceeding \$17 trillion]{.underline}, **[whereas
Russia-the main external threat to eu states-has a population of just
144 million and an annual gdp of only \$1.6 trillion. Moreover, nato\'s
European members together annually spend more than three times what
Russia does on defense.]{.underline}** [The idea that the
eu]{.underline} (whose roster includes two nuclear-armed powers) [lacks
the wherewithal to defend itself against a neighbor whose economy is
smaller than Italy\'s is risible.]{.underline} Nato still has ardent
defenders on both sides of the Atlantic, but they are living in the
past. The alliance played an invaluable role in containing the Soviet
Union and preventing the return of an aggressive, expansionist Germany.
But the Soviet Union is long gone, and Germany is now a liberal
democracy firmly committed to the status quo. [Nato\'s leaders have
worked overtime to devise new missions since the Berlin Wall came down,
but the alliance\'s attempts at nation building in the Balkans,
Afghanistan, and Libya have not gone well.]{.underline} Unless nato\'s
European members decide to back a U.S.-led effort to balance against
China (and it is not clear that they will or should), **[it is time for
the United States to gradually disengage from nato and turn European
security over to the Europeans by beginning a coordinated withdrawal of
U.S. military forces from Europe]{.underline}**, allowing a European
officer to serve as nato\'s supreme allied commander, [and making it
clear that the United States will no longer be Europe\'s first line of
defense.]{.underline} Washington should take these steps not with rancor
or resentment but with a sense of accomplishment [**and a commitment to
cooperate on issues on which American and European interests align, such
as climate change, counterterrorism, and the management of the world
economy.** Washington should also return to its traditional approach to
the Middle East]{.underline}. To ensure access to the energy supplies on
which the world economy depends, the United States has long sought to
prevent any country from dominating the oil-rich Persian Gulf. But until
the late 1960s, it did so by relying on the United Kingdom. After the
British withdrew, [Washington relied on regional clients, such as Iran,
Israel, and Saudi Arabia. U.S. forces stayed offshore until January
1991]{.underline}, a few months after Saddam Hussein, the leader of
Iraq, seized Kuwait. In response, the George H. W. Bush administration
assembled a coalition of states that liberated Kuwait, decimated Iraq\'s
military, and restored balance to the region. Today, [Washington\'s
primary goal in the Middle East remains preventing any country from
impeding the flow of oil to world markets]{.underline}. The region is
now deeply divided along several dimensions, with no state in a position
to dominate. Moreover, [the oil-producing states depend on revenue from
energy exports, which makes all of them eager to sell. **Maintaining a
regional balance of power should be relatively easy, therefore,
especially once the United States ends its counterproductive efforts to
remake local politics**. U.S. forces in Iraq and Syria would be
withdrawn, although the United States might still maintain
intelligence-gathering facilities, prepositioned equipment, and basing
arrangements in the region as a hedge against the need to return in the
future.]{.underline} But as it did from 1945 to 1991, **[Washington
would count on local powers to maintain a regional balance of power in
accordance with their own interests]{.underline}**. [As an offshore
balancer, the United States would establish normal relations with all
countries in the region]{.underline}, instead of having \"special
relationships\" with a few states and profoundly hostile relations with
others. [No country in the Middle East is so virtuous or vital that it
deserves unconditional U.S. suppo]{.underline}rt, and no country there
is so heinous that it must be treated as a pariah. The United States
should act as China, India, Japan, Russia, and the eu do, maintaining
normal working relationships with all states in the region-including
Iran. Among other things, **[this policy would encourage rival regional
powers to compete for U.S. support, instead of taking it for
granted.]{.underline}** For the moment, Washington should also make it
clear that it will reduce its support for local partners if they
repeatedly act in ways that undermine U.S. interests or that run
contrary to core U.S. values. [Should any state threaten to dominate the
region from within or without in the future, the United States would
help the rest balance against it, calibrating its level of effort and
local presence to the magnitude of the danger.]{.underline} With its
relationships with Europe and the Middle East right-sized and
rationalized, **[an offshore-balancing United States could focus
primarily on the country that is its only potential peer competitor and
the world\'s only other would-be regional hegemon: China.]{.underline}**
If China\'s power continues to grow, it is likely to press its neighbors
to distance themselves from Washington and accept China as the dominant
power in the Asia-Pacific. Were China to become a regional hegemon in
Asia, it would be better positioned to project power around the world
and extend its influence into the Western Hemisphere. [To counter this
possibility, the United States should maintain and deepen its current
security ties with Australia, Japan, the Philippines, and South Korea
and continue to nurture its strategic partnerships with India,
Singapore, and Vietnam.]{.underline} Once the United States is no longer
subsidizing its wealthy European allies or squandering trillions of
dollars on costly quagmires in the greater Middle East, it can more
readily afford the military capabilities needed to balance China.
Maintaining an effective Asian coalition will not be easy, however.
[Washington\'s Asian allies are separated from one another by water and
vast distances, and they are reluctant to jeopardize their commercial
ties with China]{.underline}. The relationship between Japan and South
Korea has a troubled history that makes close cooperation difficult.
Local powers will be tempted to let Washington do most of the work, and
sophisticated U.S. leadership will be necessary to hold this coalition
together and ensure that each member contributes its fair share.
Trump\'s missteps abandoning the Trans-Pacific Partnership, starting
trade disputes with Japan and South Korea, and indulging in an
amateurish flirtation with North Korea-have not helped. Defenders of the
status quo will no doubt mischaracterize this course of action as a
return to isolationism. That is nonsense. [As an offshore balancer, the
United States would be deeply engaged diplomatically, economically, and,
in some areas, militarily]{.underline}. It would still possess the
world\'s mightiest armed forces, even if it spent somewhat less money on
them. **[The United States would continue to work with other countries
to address major global issues such as climate change, terrorism, and
cyberthreats]{.underline}**. [But Washington would no longer assume
primary responsibility for defending wealthy allies that can defend
themselves]{.underline}, no longer subsidize client states whose actions
undermine U.S. interests, and no longer try to spread democracy via
regime change, covert action, or economic pressure. [Instead, Washington
would use its strength primarily to uphold the balance of power in
Asia-where a substantial U.S. presence]{.underline} is still needed-and
would devote more time, attention, and resources to restoring the
foundations of U.S. power at home. **[By setting an example that others
would once again admire and seek to emulate, an offshorebalancing United
States would also do a better job of promoting the political values that
Americans espouse.]{.underline}** This approach would also involve less
reliance on force and coercion and a renewed emphasis on diplomacy.
[Military power would remain central to U.S. national security, but its
use would be as a last resort rather than a first impulse]{.underline}.
It is worth remembering that some of Washington\'s greatest foreign
policy achievements- [the Marshall Plan, the Bretton Woods system, the
Egyptian-Israeli peace treaty, and the peaceful reunification of
Germany-were diplomatic victories, not battlefield ones.]{.underline} In
recent years, however, both Democratic and Republican administrations
have tended to eschew genuine diplomacy and have relied instead on
ultimatums and pressure. Convinced they hold all the high cards, too
many U.S. officials have come to see even modest concessions to
opponents as tantamount to surrender. So [they have tried to dictate
terms to others and have reached for sanctions or the sword when the
target state has refused to comply.]{.underline} But even weak states
are reluctant to submit to blackmail, and imposing one-sided agreements
on others makes them more likely to cheat or renege as soon as they can.
**[For diplomacy to work, both sides must get some of what they
want.]{.underline}** Moreover, offshore balancing requires a
sophisticated understanding of regional politics, which only
knowledgeable diplomats and area specialists can provide. [In
particular, creating an effective coalition to check China\'s ambitions
in Asia will be as much a diplomatic task as a military
mission]{.underline}, **[and success would depend on a deep bench of
officials who are intimately familiar with the history, languages,
cultures, and sensitivities of the region.]{.underline}** A return to
offshore balancing should also be accompanied by a major effort to
rebuild and professionalize the U.S. diplomatic corps. [Ambassadorships
should be reserved for qualified diplomats rather than vips or campaign
donors, and the State Department must develop, refine, and update its
diplomatic doctrine-the ways the United States can use noncoercive means
of influence]{.underline}-much as the armed services continually refine
the military doctrines that guide their conduct in war. [The ranks of
the Foreign Service should be significantly increased, and as their
careers advance, career diplomats should receive the same opportunities
for professional education that senior military officers currently
enjoy.]{.underline}

### Offshore Balancing Good -- Asia -- India

#### India is the lynchpin of US offshore balancing in Asia. The US and India have mutual interests in containing Chinese expansionism in the Indo-Asia-Pacific region and possess the military and strategic capability of checking rising hegemons.

**Paul 19** Joshy Paul is an Assistant Professor, Department of
International Studies and History, Christ \["US and India: Emerging
offshore balancing in Asia," *India Review*, Volume 18, 2019 - Issue 3,
7-8-2019, DOI: <https://doi.org/10.1080/14736489.2019.1616258>\] DTS

University, Bangalore Obama's pivot to Asia: Forging a strong bilateral
relationship with India [The declaration of a "US pivot to Asia" in 2011
by the Obama administration was a major change in US policy toward
Asia]{.underline}. Through the pivot or the "rebalancing," the
[Obama]{.underline} administration **[tried to forge new partnerships in
the region which include India, Indonesia, and Vietnam]{.underline}**.38
With the growing challenge from China in mind, the Obama administration
**[clearly indicated the US intention to invest in a "long-term
strategic partnership" with India]{.underline}** so that [New Delhi
might serve as a "regional economic anchor and provider of security in
the broader Indian Ocean region]{.underline}." 39 The Obama
administration clearly expressed its desire to prop-up India as a
regional counterweight to China in Asia.40 After initial reluctance to
endorse the pivot, [New Delhi has gradually embraced the idea, given the
nature of China's intentions to encircle India in the Indian
Ocean]{.underline} and US readiness to recognize India's strategic
positioning in the Indo--Pacific region. In its new maritime strategy of
2015, [the US deliberately used the term Indo--Asia--Pacific instead of
the traditional "Asia-Pacific]{.underline}" and "recognized the
Indo--Asia--Pacific as a single geopolitical theater with multiple
centers of gravity and regional leaders" where India was placed squarely
in the "center of that strategy." 41 Given the context [of China's
increasing assertiveness in various territorial disputes, its ambitions
to emerge as a great power in Asia, and its naval expansionism in the
Indian Ocean region,]{.underline} a synergy of interest has emerged
between the US and India in the twenty-first century. **[The US had
initially considered India along with Japan and Australia to help
promote its interest in Asia strategically and
militarily.]{.underline}** Strategically, it expected India to join the
balancing mechanism against China along with other allies and partners
which considered China threat to their security. However, India was
reluctant to participate in a US led alliance mechanism because that
would be perceived as anti-China. As its largest neighbor and a superior
power in terms of both traditional and nuclear war capability, New Delhi
does not want to view China as a permanent enemy in its backyard.
Considering the dissent articulated by China in 2007, India was even
reluctant to include Japan in the Malabar exercise until 2015.42
Similarly [New Delhi]{.underline} was apprehensive about Japanese Prime
Minister Shinzo Abe's "Security Diamond" concept of 2012,43 as it would
be considered anti-China, but [grabbed the opportunity to become an
active participant in the new "Quadrilateral Dialog" mechanism because
of China's aggressive expansionist tendencies in the Indian
Ocean]{.underline}.44 **[India has shown growing interest in building
bilateral partnerships with the US.]{.underline}** The
[Obama]{.underline} administration [elevated bilateral
cooperation]{.underline} to the next level, [adding an economic
advantage to the strategic cooperation]{.underline} for both countries.
For any defense or strategic cooperation to sustain for long, it has to
generate economic benefits for both partners in the form of trade in
military hardware and technology cooperation, including the joint
development of systems. For instance, a major factor behind the success
of Indo-- Russian strategic relations is the trade in military hardware,
as India is the largest importer of arms from Russia. Similarly, [in
Indo--US relations, India seeks greater technological collaboration
rather than a mere buy-and-sell type of relationship with the
US]{.underline}. The Obama administration understood India's reluctance
to enter into a formal alliance with the US, but wanted to strengthen
defense technology cooperation while continuing its "strategic autonomy"
policy. **[In this pursuit, the US and India signed a Defense Trade and
Technology Initiative]{.underline}** (DTTI) agreement in 2012 [aimed at
strengthening cooperation in research, co-production, and co-development
of capabilities needed for sustaining and modernizing India military
forces]{.underline}. Under this initiative four "pathfinder projects"
were identified: next generation Raven Minis UAVs, roll-on and roll- off
kits for C-130s, mobile electric hybrid power sources, and Uniform
Integrated Protection Ensemble Increment-2; and, [the two countries have
agreed to the joint production of fighter aircrafts by Lockheed Martin
and Boeing]{.underline}.45 In fact, in the early stages of his
presidency President [Obama]{.underline} did not consider China an enemy
and sought better engagement with Beijing. He [pursued a policy of "deep
engagement" with China and a "military restraint" strategy toward the
Asia-Pacific region]{.underline}. His objective was to make China a
responsible stakeholder in the international system and to promote
"winwin cooperation" between the two countries.46 An almost decade-long
war on terror and the economic crisis of 2008 placed great economic
stress on the US and hampered its ability to maintain its military
preponderance or even compete with China in Asia. President Obama
responded to this with his initiative of a "new type of great power
relations" between China and the US in the creation of the G-2 (Group of
Two), with greater economic interaction including a massive purchase by
China of US Treasury bonds, expansion of the US-China Economic and
Strategic Dialog, and negotiations for a USChina bilateral investment
treaty (BIT) that induced an unprecedented level of economic integration
between the two countries. Conversely, [China utilized the international
environment to its advantage and became a more assertive power in the
region]{.underline}. China's continued support for the US-led war on
terror had minimized US focus on allegations of human rights violations
in China and its military modernization. During this time, the Chinese
economy continued its steady growth and the country overtook the US as
the largest economy in 2014. Taking cognizance of the upcoming challenge
in Asia from an economically strong and militarily powerful China,
President Obama responded with his strategic "pivot to Asia," announced
by his Secretary of State Hillary Clinton at the ASEAN Regional Forum in
Hanoi on July 23, 2010, which was aimed at containing China. [The chief
component of the "pivot," and later the "rebalancing," was to deploy 60
percent of the US Navy and Air Force in the Asia-Pacific region
vis-√†-vis the Atlantic]{.underline}, and create an economic grouping
called the TransPacific Partnership (TPP) comprising 12 countries from
the Pacific belt, excluding China. [China responded to the rebalancing
with more aggressive tactics by creating a rival regional economic
groupings called]{.underline} the Regional Comprehensive Economic
Partnership ([RECP)]{.underline} and the Free Trade Area of the
Asia-Pacific ([FTAAP), as well as]{.underline} introducing its "[One
Belt, One Road]{.underline}" initiative, [and]{.underline} leading the
creation of the [BRICS Development Bank and Asian Infrastructure
Investment Ban]{.underline}k.47 **[As a result, the US pivot was not
able to contain China, nor did China become the fully "responsible"
global power that the US had visualized.]{.underline}** The Obama
administration could not continue the pace of the pivot because of its
domestic economic constraints. This resulted in Beijing taking a more
assertive position in staking claims in the East China and South China
seas and reacting more sharply to the assertion of claims by others.
President Obama's Air-Sea Battle doctrine deemed to counter China's
A2/AD did not become operationally successful and had to be abandoned.48
The introduction of a "sequestration" policy in 2012, which lowered US
defense spending by 10 percent (in 2013) to 8.5 percent (in 2021), a
total of \$454 billion over a period of ten years, compounded the
problem further.49 Additionally, the US could not stop China's rise.
President Obama's second term had a major shift in policy toward Asia.
China became more assertive under President Xi Jinping's leadership
after a smooth transition of power at the top. [The number of skirmishes
between China and its neighboring countries increased, with its
declaration of an air defense identification zone in the East China
Sea]{.underline}, its expansion of Tree Island and North Island in the
Paracels, as well as building of artificial islands in the South China
Sea, [turning them into military and logistical bases]{.underline}.50
However, the US is not in a position to intervene in all these disputes
unless they directly threaten US interests in the region. **[As a
result, the Obama administration started building stronger military,
diplomatic, and economic ties with allies and other regional partners,
although it did not explicitly convey its intentions to contain
China]{.underline}**.51 In fact, the Obama administration made a
concerted effort to expand the concept of Asia-Pacific to include the
Indian Ocean, in order to include in India in its rebalancing strategy,
without which, it realized, rebalancing would not be successful.52 India
was also looking to balance China in collaboration with the US. [A major
aspect in the relationship is that the Obama administration sought to
institutionalize US' strategic relationship with India and ensured
bipartisan consensus]{.underline} in the American Congress for
India-specific defense-related agreements. **[The DTTI was a major step
in this direction and enabled defense cooperation and collaboration for
the joint production and transfer of technology]{.underline}**.
Considering the importance of India in the US calculation of a security
order in Asia, [the US signed a Logistics Exchange Memorandum of
Agreement (LEMOA) with India in August 2016]{.underline}. Indeed, the
LEMOA is an India-specific pact and differs from the text of the
original Logistic Support Agreements (LSAs), which the US has signed
with more than 60 countries[. It took a decade to sign this major
customized agreement, as discussions on the pact had started in 2003,
and it was one of the three "foundational pacts" that the US wanted
India to sign]{.underline}: the Logistic Support Agreement (LSA), the
Communications Interoperability & Security Memorandum of Agreement
(CISMOA), and the Basic Exchange & Cooperation Agreement on geo-spatial
services (BECA) for defense technology cooperation. Former US Defense
Secretary Ashton Carter noted after the signing of the LEMOA that this
kind of agreement "[we do only with our closest and most long-standing
allies. That's a very big change]{.underline}. ...... It's an enormous
change from 50 years of US history." 53 Indeed, the LEMO Agreement
propelled New Delhi autonomy. [It allowed both nations to use their
military facilities in times of crises and in peace-time
operations]{.underline}, with the larger goal of enhancing
interoperability. Similarly, the US' National Defense Authorization Act
(NDAA) 2017 incorporated a specific India clause, which was approved by
the House of Representative in May 2016 and the Senate in December 2016,
which brought India on par with its NATO allies in terms of the sale of
defense equipment and technology transfer.54 Section 1292 of the [NDAA
mandated the Secretary of Defense and Secretary of State to recognize
India as a "Major Defense Partner]{.underline}" of the US; to designate
an individual within the executive branch to supervise the
implementation of the Framework for the U.S.--India Defense Relationship
and help resolve remaining issues impeding defense trade, security
cooperation, and co-production and co-development opportunities; and to
approve and facilitate the transfer of advanced technology, consistent
with the US' conventional arms transfer policy.55 [If a strategic
relationship needs to be strengthened, it must be supported by economic
dividends]{.underline}. The Indo--US arms trade deal made enormous
progress during President Obama's tenure and at one stage the US had
become the largest exporter of arms to India. India's arms purchases
from the US during 2001--4 was merely \$400 million which expanded to
\$3.2 billion during the 2005--8 period (see Table 1).56 After the
nuclear deal was signed and after President Obama came to power in 2008,
there has been significant improvement in defense dealings between the
two countries: [bilateral defense trade increased to \$5.74 billion in
2013, to \$12 billion in 2014, and by the time President Obama left
office it was over \$14 billion.57]{.underline} After Prime Minister
Narendra Modi came to power, [the Indian government signed another deal
for \$2.5 billion for US-made Boeing CH-47 Chinook and Boeing AH-64
Apache helicopters in 2015.58]{.underline} In 2015, the Indian
government approved a \$3-billion deal for Boeing's 22 Apache attack
choppers and 15 Chinook cargo choppers; the Chinooks will be bought
directly from Boeing, while the Apache contract would take place through
the foreign militarysales procedure.59 In total \$8.3 billion worth of
arms purchase agreements have been signed between the two countries.60
India became the second-biggest buyer of US arms after Saudi Arabia and
the US even replaced Russia as the largest arms exporter to India during
the 2011--14 period.61 India has purchased more than \$15 billion in US
defense equipment in the past decade and plans to spend over \$150
billion over the next ten to fifteen years, a significant portion on US
equipment.62 **[The Trump administration continues the previous
administration's policy of a strategic partnership with
India]{.underline}** and is ready to accommodate India's desire to waive
over the two foundation pacts: -- [the Communication and Information
Security Memorandum of Agreement (CISMOA) and the Basic Exchange and
Cooperation Agreement (BECA]{.underline}) -- which would enable the
transfer of critical defense technologies to India as well as the trade
in arms, by waiving these pacts. In the National Defense Authorization
Act (NDAA) 2018 for the 2018--19 fiscal period, the [Trump
administration proposed an "advance defense cooperation with India,"
which includes joint exercises, operations, and patrols and mutual
defense planning,]{.underline} a step ahead of the NDAA-2017.63 The
Trump administration is considering supplying armed drones for the
Indian Air Force and sophisticated Electromagnetic Aircraft Launch
Systems (EMALS) for the Indian Navy's future aircraft carriers; the
former will help India counter cross-border terrorism from Pakistan,
[while the latter is required for India's third aircraft carrier being
built indigenously at Cochin Shipyard.]{.underline} It is a major
development in Indo--US relations, as India can now cooperate with US
defense contractors without signing the two basic pacts. Earlier, India
was noncommittal to sign customized agreements and sought "special
treatment" from the US, which the US Senate could not easily agree to.
[The Trump administration said that the President "strongly supports"
the sale of F-18 and F-16 fighter jets to India]{.underline} and
asserted that the proposals have the potential to take Indo--US defense
ties to the next level.64 Washington expressed its desires to sell India
Boeing's F/A-18E/F aircrafts and is ready to set up a plant to build the
fighter in India; it expect bilateral defense trade to reach \$18--25
billion from the current \$15--18 billion.65 The Trump administration
accords high priority to its relationship with India as a "Major Defense
Partner" and is ready to expand its existing defense and security
cooperation further.66 More importantly, it provides "support to India's
leadership role in Indian Ocean security and throughout the broader
region," 67 which is a clear indication of the US offshore balancing
strategy in Trump's Indo--Pacific strategy. "Act-east" converges with
the "pivot" **[There has been a convergence of the interests of India
and the US in recent times with regard to the security situation in the
Indo--Pacific]{.underline}**. The two countries have identified
"maritime security as a key area of cooperation" in the coming decades,
in the 2016 joint statement.68 Maritime cooperation between the two
countries began in 1992 when they launched the Malabar series of naval
exercise, and has now become trilateral to include Japan. Indian Prime
Minister Narendra Modi recently elevated India's look-east policy to an
"act-east" policy, making India a robust "actor" in the security
apparatus of the Indo--Pacific. [India has now become an active
participant in various security mechanisms concerning the Indo--Pacific,
which include the ASEAN Regional Forum (ARF), East Asia Summit (EAS),
and ADMM Plus. **Naval engagement between the two nations has become a
routine procedure at various levels, such as the Malabar
exercises**]{.underline}, Rim of the Pacific (RIMPAC) exercises and
anti-piracy operations in the Gulf of Aden. **[More importantly, India
and the US share the perception that China's emergence as a great power
in the region would disrupt freedom of navigation in the
Indio-Pacific,]{.underline}** especially in the South China Sea.
Undoubtedly, New Delhi wants to emerge as a strong maritime power in the
Indian Ocean region, and is expanding its influence to the western
Pacific. India is willing to back the US on its pivot strategy, but has
made it clear that it is not open to the idea of becoming an American
ally.69 At the same time, [New Delhi is enhancing its bilateral
strategic partnership with friendly East Asian countries such as Japan,
Vietnam, South Korea, Indonesia, and Singapore]{.underline}, so-called
allies of the US. India is building up an advanced force structure in
the Indian Ocean and expects the US to support this in terms of
high-level defense technology. **[At the same time, both countries can
work together to protect their mutual interests, such as freedom of
navigation, disaster management, domain awareness and, above all,
countering China's dominance in the Indo--Pacific region.]{.underline}**
The convergence of the "pivot" and "act east" happens at two levels:
strategic and operational. [At the strategic level, the US and India
share a broad strategic interest in setting limits to China's
geopolitical horizons]{.underline} and working together to support
mutually reinforcing goals without becoming "allies" in the traditional
sense of the balancing.70 To validate this, the [defense cooperation has
reached at the higher levels and the US has conveyed its readiness to
address New Delhi's quest for increasingly sophisticated
technologies.]{.underline} The 2005 Framework agreement set the platform
for a joint mechanism in a mutually beneficial manner. One of the thrust
areas of the pivot is the US interest in encouraging and assisting
regional states in developing capabilities to deal with shared
challenges.71 The former US Defense Secretary Ashton Carter projected
India as the biggest and most dynamic country in this region and has
expressed the US' commitment to upgrading the current level of defense
cooperation, especially from that of a "buyer and seller" to a more
substantial one, in which the US will share important cutting-edge
technologies, co-production and joint research and development, which
will include jet engines, aircraft carrier design, and construction, and
other areas.72 Importantly, of late the US has begun to recognize
India's leadership position in the Indian Ocean region, which gives them
greater energy and time to target the western Pacific including the
South China Sea. In his first visit to India, US Defense Secretary James
Mattis acknowledged India's standing in the Indo--Pacific, stating [that
"India has a vital role to play in supporting South East Asia's regional
institutions -- particularly ASEAN --]{.underline} and in building
partner capacity across the region. We appreciate India's leadership in
the Indian Ocean and we seek to work together to build a resilient
regional architecture." 73 **[With India's strong leadership in the
Indian Ocean region in tandem with the US preponderance in the western
Pacific, and the support of US allies and other Southeast Asian
countries, Chinese hegemonic ambitions could be thwarted.]{.underline}**
Operationally, the cooperation is growing largely in the maritime
domain. The Maritime Security Cooperation Framework of 2006 enhanced
mutual understanding of the challenges both countries face in this
domain. The areas identified for potential collaboration were the
prevention of transnational crime at sea; safety of ships, crew, and
property as well of navigation; and prevention of illicit trafficking in
weapons of mass destruction, their delivery systems, and related
materials. [A major success of this maritime cooperation has been the
manner in which the countries conduct joint naval
exercises]{.underline}; in fact, India conducts more exercises with the
US than with any other country.74 Malabar started as a modest table-top
exercise but now includes advanced platforms, such destroyers and
aircraft carriers, and conducting war games. **[Recently, India and the
US have decided to implement an agreement to improve interoperability
between their defense forces, specially their navies, called Helicopter
Operations from Ships Other Than Aircraft Carriers
(HOSTAC).]{.underline}**75 Cooperation between the two navies continues
at a steady pace as both military establishments continue to build
people-topeople ties, conduct exercises, and attend professional
military courses. However, India is still reluctant to participate in
any joint operations with the US Navy as New Delhi has not endorsed a US
proposal to conduct joint patrols in the South China Sea, though both
countries affirm the importance of freedom of navigation in the
region.76 Since both countries pursue different strategies, philosophies
and operational procedures, perhaps joint operations is the one area in
which they would face the most difficulty. At the same time, both
countries need to overcome the political obstacles that the US faces
from India77 and the technical regulations that constrains the US from
forging a deep defence engagement with India. [Through maritime
cooperation, India expects to receive advanced systems from the US, in
terms of hardware as well as advanced technology, which would help it
keep the Indian Ocean as free]{.underline} and open as it was at the
time of US supremacy in the region at the time of the Cold War.
Conclusion **[The Indo--US relationship has become an important
component of the foreign policies of both the US and
India]{.underline}**: for the US, India has a role as a trusted partner
in its security strategy toward the Indo--Pacific, and for India, US
support is necessary to enable it to emerge as a "credible power" in the
international system. [The US' main concern is China's increasing
assertiveness in the East and South China seas]{.underline}, wherein
none of the regional powers have the wherewithal to withstand China. As
a result, the US has to remain a preponderant power, to maintain the
balance of power in the western Pacific. At the same time, [the
US]{.underline} cannot deploy its forces simultaneously in the western
Pacific and the Indian Ocean theaters. It [requires a trusted partner in
the Indian Ocean region, so it can focus on the western Pacific in order
to keep its security assurance to its allies and to counter
China.]{.underline} **[An offshore balancing strategy in the Indian
Ocean will help the US give greater attention to the western Pacific
while its interests in the Indian Ocean will not be adversely affected
because of the presence of the offshore balancer.]{.underline}**
Undoubtedly, a militarily strong India can counter China in the Indian
Ocean region and ensure freedom of navigation in the water body.
**[Based on current progress in Indo--US relations, India could become
the true offshore balancer for the US in Asia.]{.underline}** However,
to sustain the offshore balancing mechanism and to enable it to
contribute to the future stability of the Indo--Pacific, the
relationship must be based on mutually acceptable terms and an equal
partnership. Ever since its independence, self-reliance has been the
bedrock of India's foreign and security policy. It has passed through
various periods with varying nomenclature, starting with Nehruvian
idealism and the nonaligned movement (NAM), a Soviet tilt during the
1970s and 1980s without being a follower of the Soviet system, and
strategic autonomy under the United Progressive Alliance government
which made it a 'credible' power in the international system. It has now
turned to the "make in India" program to reduce its dependence on
foreign-made defense systems, while making the nation self-sufficient,
as well as enabling it to export advanced military hardware. India
expects have continued technological support for its nascent but
thriving arms industry from the US. It does not want to be part of a
client-patron relationship with any foreign country, and wants to emerge
a truly independent and credible power in the international system. In
the changing security dynamics of Asia, India believes that close
cooperation with the US would fulfill its ambitions of being a major
power in the international system without diluting its independence and
autonomy. At the same time, convergence in the security policies of the
two countries in the Indian Ocean could be extended to other parts of
the Indo--Pacific. [The Indo--US offshore balancing mechanism could
establish a strong foundation for the evolving Quadrilateral Mechanism
(QUAD), comprising the US, India, Japan, and Australia]{.underline},
which is also based on strong bilateral relationships between all four
countries. The success of the QUAD also depends on how far India and
Japan can resist China with the support of the US in their respective
regions -- India in the Indian Ocean and Japan in the East China Sea. In
this regard, **[the offshore balancing mechanism in the Indo--US
relationship will contribute to enduring peace and stability in the
Indo--Pacific.]{.underline}**

### Offshore Balancing Good -- Asia -- Generic

#### Offshore balancing is key to Asian stability. China's A2AD strategy and development of the D21DF missile prevents US naval deployments in the region. The US needs to reinvigorate regional allies like India, Japan, and Australia to check against revisionist China.

**Paul 19** Joshy Paul is an Assistant Professor, Department of
International Studies and History, Christ \["US and India: Emerging
offshore balancing in Asia," *India Review*, Volume 18, 2019 - Issue 3,
7-8-2019, DOI: <https://doi.org/10.1080/14736489.2019.1616258>\] DTS

US offshore balancing in Asia The balance of power in the Asia-Pacific
was maintained largely by US military preponderance,9 but **[the US now
faces difficulty in projecting its power hegemonistically in
Asia]{.underline}**. During the Cold War, northeast Asia was the major
theater of the US balancing mechanism in Asia, which was also very much
part of the power rivalry between the US and the former Soviet Union[.
Regional countries like Japan and South Korea allowed stationing US
troops both for preserving their own sovereignty and to protect US
interests in the region]{.underline}. However, **[none of them worked as
offshore balancers because of inherent constrains -- Japan's pacifist
constitution and South Korea's lack of military
capability.]{.underline}** In fact, the US created an alliance system in
Asia with the aim of exerting maximum control over smaller allies'
actions.10 [In the case of Japan, the US intended to prevent Tokyo from
reinvigorating its power in the future.]{.underline} Importantly, during
the Cold War period there was no regional hegemon to challenge US power
in Asia, and it enjoyed an asymmetric military power equation with both
its allies and potential enemies. But today, [China fits well into the
category of a regional hegemon to counter the US position in Asia.
**China enjoys conventional military superiority in the mainland of
northeast Asia,**]{.underline} while the US is still a distant power
even though it is the predominant maritime power in the western Pacific.
In a confrontation between a land power and a maritime power, each
side's military strengths are at a disadvantage in the others'
theater.11 [To counter US military advancement to its shores, China has
developed an anti-access/area denial (A2/AD) strategy that would allow
Beijing to obstruct the arrival of additional US military units and
limit the effectiveness of forward deployed forces in the western
Pacific]{.underline}.12 The **[development of the D21DF missile which is
targeted to strike aircraft carrier groups outside the first island
chain has led to a rethinking in the overall US strategy toward the
Pacific]{.underline}**. Although China's military budget is only about a
third of America's, if present trends continue the gap will quickly
narrow.13 A few years ago, The Economist projected that China's defense
spending could overtake US spending after 2035.14 Undoubtedly, [China's
emergence would badly affect US supremacy in the Indo--Pacific
region.]{.underline}15 In this regard, [US policy]{.underline} toward
the Indo--Pacific [is now centered on three]{.underline} different, but
complimenting [strategies: deep engagement with China, active defense,
and offshore balancing.]{.underline} Although the US continues deep
engagement with China at various levels, the great power rivalry, as
offensive realists predict, cannot be avoided.16 The US and China are
destined to be rivals for supremacy in Asia. [The US employs an "active
defense" strategy to defeat the Chinese threat at its
origin]{.underline}, for which [it pursues a preponderance and forward
deployment of naval assets in the western Pacific]{.underline} where
China's threat is at its maximum.17 The US wants to ensure its own
preeminent position in the western Pacific and the sovereignty of its
trusted allies. At the same time, [it cannot simultaneously focus on the
Pacific and the Indian oceans, otherwise it would cripple its military
preponderance in the western Pacific.]{.underline} In this regard,
[**the US engages in an offshore balancing strategy toward the Indian
Ocean region.** Offensive realism suggests that China should be
countered effectively at the local level otherwise]{.underline} the US
would have to step in and balance against the rising power.18
Mearsheimer argues that [distant hegemons usually prefer to let the
local great powers check an aspiring hegemon with outside
support]{.underline}.19 **[The regional states can balance against a
potential hegemon and the incumbent hegemon can confidently shape
alliance relationships with the local powers which favor an offshore
balancing strategy]{.underline}**.20 [The best way for the US to deter
dangerous rivals]{.underline} in other regions from pursuing regional
hegemony [is to rely on local powers to counter aspiring
hegemons]{.underline}, otherwise it would have to keep its military
forces ready over the horizon. Indeed, **[an offshore balancing strategy
against China becomes necessary for the US]{.underline}** for two
reasons; first, the deepening financial crisis will compel retrenchment
in the US, which will be increasingly less able to invest in its
military; and, second, as ascending powers such as China become
wealthier, their military expenditures will expand.21 Christopher Layne
argues that an [offshore balancing strategy would be]{.underline} one of
the best options for the US, as it is [considerably less expensive than
a strategy of preponderance against China]{.underline}.22 An [offshore
balancing]{.underline} strategy **[would also allow it to preserve its
dominance in the Western Hemisphere, while countering potential hegemons
in Europe, Indo-- Pacific, and the Persian Gulf, and would encourage
secondary states to take the lead in checking rising
powers,]{.underline}** leaving the US to intervene only when
necessary.23 However, if the local power is threatened or China really
changes the status quo by utilizing military means to settle territorial
disputes that China has involved in with the neighboring countries then
the US can intervene militarily in the region. Under offshore balancing,
[the US can propel a local power, which could become the first line of
defense, letting them uphold the balance of power in their own
neighborhood]{.underline}, as they have a far greater interest in
preventing a state from dominating them. Washington could provide
assistance for their military modernisation to thwart the threat from
the rising hegemon**[. This could be through the supply of sophisticated
defense systems, technological cooperation, and joint
exercises]{.underline}**. [Defense technology cooperation would be
advantageous in helping the local power establish a domestic defense
infrastructure system and enabling them to grow faster.]{.underline} In
the process, **[both countries would develop greater synergy in their
security policies]{.underline}**, which would expand beyond the regional
level. The [defense cooperation would be mutually beneficial and
enduring]{.underline} as it would address a common threat. In offshore
balancing, the local power enjoys greater autonomy compared with a
traditional alliance system, as the local power has a minimal amount of
commitment to the great power's other global interests. **[The local
power is more interested in deterring the rising hegemon in its
backyard.]{.underline}** With the help of the great power, [it can
emerge strong enough to take on the rising hegemon independently and can
also manage regional security in its backyar]{.underline}d. Thus, once
the offshore balancing starts functioning effectively, the great power
can gradually withdraw from the region. [The US has always showed keen
interest in preserving the status quo in Asia]{.underline}. As far back
as a 100 years ago, renowned American geostrategist Alfred Thayer Mahan
put forth the argument that the security of an emergent US was
intimately tied to the Asian balance of power as well as to that of
Europe.24 Eminent Australian strategist Paul Dibb predicted in 1995 that
[India had "greater potential as a power of influence" and "could emerge
as a useful player in a multipolar Asian balance]{.underline}" and
"attract American interest as a counterbalance to China." 25 [India
traditionally follows democratic principles and human rights values,
does not pursue great power ambition]{.underline}, and fits the
requirements of an offshore balancer in Asia to counter China.
[**India's military modernisation is not aimed at establishing hegemony
in South Asia but at countering the threat from China and its proxy
Pakistan.** Today, India has acquired military capabilities to target
all the major cities in China]{.underline}. **[New Delhi has also
acquired second-strike nuclear capability from three levels: air, land,
and sea]{.underline}**.26 However, with a deep-rooted sense of pride and
nationalism in its strategic thinking, New Delhi wants to become a
respected, credible, and independent power in the international system,
not a junior partner to the US in Asia

### Offshore Balancing Good -- Middle East

#### America First's offshore balancing in the Middle East prevents neoconservative interventions. Rather than global policing, offshore balancing ensures regional stability by spurring regional security and diplomatic agreements.

**Parpiani 18** Kashish Parpiani is a Research Fellow at ORF's Mumbai
Centre. \["Trump's gateway to offshore balancing," *Observer Research
Foundation*, 12-26-2018, URL:
<https://www.orfonline.org/expert-speak/trumps-gateway-to-offshore-balancing-46498/>\]
DTS

[The Trump administration's recent decisions to withdraw US troops from
Syria]{.underline}, have once again sparked a debate on the United
States' role in the world. In a series of recent tweets, President
Donald Trump hailed his decision to withdraw US troops [and questioned
the rationale for the US fighting counter-terrorism operations abroad.
Trump deemed the United States to have received "NOTHING but spending
precious lives and trillions of dollars protecting others]{.underline}
who, in almost all cases, do not appreciate what we are doing?"
Predictably, most American lawmakers, media, and academia gasped over
Trump's decision undercutting American commitments towards allied
Kurdish forces, ceding ground to growing Iranian influence. They warned
of this decision emboldening Russian stranglehold over the region, and
even that it may pave way for the resurgence of the Islamic State. Yet,
a small, but prominent, group of commentators and legislators hailed
Trump's decision. For instance, [noted realist scholar Stephen Walt
claimed that Trump had done "the right thing", and Republican Senator
Rand Paul -- known for his libertarian views -- announced that he was
"very proud of the President" for withdrawing from Syria.]{.underline}
Increasingly, **[calls for a more narrow conception of US interests
abroad have been growing]{.underline}**. Advocates for [US retrenchment
to a grand strategy of restraint]{.underline} often [deem **the United
States to lack "the need, the capability, and the mandate to manage
global security**]{.underline}." Moreover, [some]{.underline} even
[argue sustaining American primacy for the longer term]{.underline}
**[by husbanding resources with an offshore-balancing
strategy.]{.underline}** Stopping short of outright isolationism, such
an approach dictates, "[instead of policing the world, the United States
would encourage other countries to take the lead in checking rising
powers, intervening itself only when necessary."]{.underline} Most
importantly, this approach construes [US global leadership as "not an
end in itself]{.underline}", rather it deems the same to be "[desirable
only insofar as it benefits the United States directly." In Trump's
conduct of the United States' foreign policy, such a view holds
currency]{.underline} -- from accusing European and North American
allies of treating the US "like a piggy bank to be robbed", to
announcing other countries like Turkey to take the lead in fighting the
Islamic State. In addition, in expressing deep aversion on
nation-building activities abroad, senior Trump administration officials
have announced that [the United States will no longer pursue its
"longstanding pattern of aid without effect, assistance without
accountability, and relief without reform]{.underline}." One may look no
further than Pakistan with regards to evidence supporting that shift in
policy, as **[the Trump administration holds back aid amounting to over
\$1 billion, and supports Pakistan's grey-listing at multilateral
platforms like the Financial Action Task Force]{.underline}**.
Furthermore, on husbanding resources, the [Trump]{.underline}
administration's [repeated outcry over seeking "fair" and "reciprocal
deals" has even pulled into Trump's cross-fire countries like
India]{.underline} -- notwithstanding the fact that India takes the
tenth spot on the list of countries that register a deficit with the US
and supposedly enjoys broad bipartisan support amongst US policymakers
and national-security officials. Further, although [the
transactionalism]{.underline} witnessed in recent American foreign
policy [can, in large parts, be attributed to Trump, a slow shift away
from the standard pre-set for liberal internationalism]{.underline} has
been evident in the post-Cold War world. With continued American primacy
-- chiefly in the military realm, at its core, the immediate aftermath
of the Cold War under President George H. W. [Bush hailed the emergence
of "a new world order, where brutality will go unrewarded and aggression
will meet collective resistance]{.underline}." Thereafter, the Bill
[Clinton]{.underline} years [saw]{.underline} the [employment of US
force for a myriad range of non-combat missions]{.underline} -- across
the Balkans and Sub-Saharan Africa, [under the liberal internationalist
grand strategy of "enlargement" of democratic regimes]{.underline}
mainly in the areas of the former Soviet Union. This inclination towards
employing the US military as the 'tip of the spear' towards US foreign
policy interests, reached its peak under the subsequent George W. Bush
years. Armed by a neoconservative outlook and American military primacy
riding high in the post-9/11 fog of war, the [Bush administrations
pursued regime-change agendas primarily in the Middle East]{.underline}.
Subsequently, an American standing -- tarnished by the Bush-era's
militarised unilateralism -- fed the [Obama]{.underline}
administrations' motivation behind 'leading from behind', [encouraging
burden-sharing, cobbling multilateral coalitions across varied realms of
nuclear non-proliferation]{.underline}, combating terrorist safe-havens,
and even humanitarian interventions. Finally, [the trend away from
liberal internationalism]{.underline} seems to have **[reached its
farthest point on non-intervention and minimal US stewardship with the
Trump presidency.]{.underline}** By April 2016, support for American
activism abroad had dwindled. According to a poll, about 57 percent of
Americans agreed that the United States should "deal with its own
problems and let others deal with theirs the best they can." In the
near-future, charged by nationalist overtones of a renewed conservative
movement, the '**[America First' outlook may just usher in the last mile
towards offshore-balancing.]{.underline}** Many see the shuttering of
The Weekly Standard -- run by noted neoconservative commentator Bill
Kirstol, as an ominous sign of the shift away from the Reagan era
conservatism of touting the United States' perceived stature as the
"shining city upon a hill". Moreover, commentators have even noted
[Trump's conservative nationalism of 'America First\' to have forced the
marginalised liberal internationalists and Bush-era neoconservatives to
find common ground despite their divergences]{.underline} over
employment of force and multilateralism. Further, [increasingly younger
American generations seem to have a greater preference for a less global
America]{.underline}, as revealed by a recent Pew Research polls. One
poll found [younger Americans (under 30)]{.underline} [to
be]{.underline} generally [less likely to prioritise limiting
power]{.underline} and influence [of foreign powers like Russia, and
China]{.underline} -- only three-in-ten young people concurred, while 54
percent (65 or older) argued for the US to actively engage in limiting
the influence of Russia. Another poll found a substantial age divide in
the priority given to maintaining US military primacy -- 64 percent
older Americans concurring vs. 30 percent younger Americans. Reduction
of American military commitments abroad drew support of 34 percent
younger Americans vs. 20 percent older Americans. On combatting
terrorism as well, younger generations seemed to support less US
military activism. When asked if the US should actively take measures
towards seeking and destroying terror groups in other countries, only 27
percent of Americans under 50 deemed it as a top priority compared to 44
percent of those 50 and older. Thus, future generations' preferences
against US activism abroad compounded by [the rise of Trumpian
conservative nationalism, seems poised to challenge the post-Cold War
dominance of liberal internationalism in American strategic
culture.]{.underline}

### Offshore Balancing Good -- Europe

#### Trump and a growing body of domestic support are beginning offshore balancing in Europe. Offshore balancing maintains European peace despite Russia. Russia's defensive posture and declining GDP make a US led NATO obsolete

**Bandow 19** 19 Doug Bandow is a senior fellow at the Cato Institute
and a former special assistant to President Ronald Reagan. \["The
Outdated Alliance?," *Foreign Affairs*, 4-3-2019, URL:
<https://foreignpolicy.com/2019/04/03/the-outdated-alliance/>\] DTS

[Washington is filled with sacred cows. And none is more sacrosanct than
NATO.]{.underline} On the organization's 70th anniversary this week,
encomiums fill the air for "the strongest, most successful alliance in
history," as NATO Secretary-General Jens Stoltenberg put it. [At least
one person in Washington disagrees: U.S. President Donald
Trump]{.underline}. And although the State Department has affirmed that
"the U.S. commitment ... to NATO remains firm," **[the organization's
future is far from certain]{.underline}**, and this year's birthday
celebration is rather subdued. [When NATO was formed seven decades ago,
the world was very different]{.underline}: The Soviet Union had advanced
into Central Europe, and Western European nations were still recovering
from World War II. NATO would help them, Secretary of State Dean Acheson
warned, but it would not do so forever[. When asked if the United States
would need "to send substantial numbers of troops over there as a more
or less permanent contribution," he assured Congress that it
wouldn't]{.underline}. Even Dwight Eisenhower, NATO's first commander
and a future U.S. president, presciently warned that such an American
garrison could "discourage the development of the necessary military
strength Western European countries should provide themselves." [**The
Europeans eventually did recover but, as predicted, lagged in** defense.
The United States has for decades demanded that European countries spend
more on defense---and they agree, only to inevitably fall
short]{.underline}. The process endlessly repeats, teaching each
generation of European leaders that no matter how little they do,
Washington will defend the continent. The dissolution of the Warsaw Pact
and the collapse of the Soviet Union in 1991 put NATO's survival in
doubt. **[To give the organization a new raison d'√™tre, officials
suggested a plethora of improbable new duties.]{.underline}** For
instance, Robert Zoellick, then-counselor to Secretary of State James
Baker, argued that it was necessary to ["transform established
institutions, such as NATO, to serve new missions that will fit the new
era."]{.underline} David Abshire, who had been the U.S. ambassador to
NATO under President Ronald Reagan, suggested that the alliance ["could
coordinate the transfer of environmental-control technology to the
East."]{.underline} Curious work for a military alliance. [NATO decided
to stay relevant in two ways.]{.underline} First[, it opted to expand
its membership to countries formerly in the Soviet orbit]{.underline}.
Second, [it opted to undertake activities in nonmember
nations]{.underline}. The former violated assurances that NATO had given
Moscow in 1990 and 1991, fomenting Russian hostility. The latter
transformed NATO into an offensive force, most notably during its
mission in Serbia in 1999. Nevertheless, as U.S. Sen. Richard Lugar
argued at the time, the choice was simple. The alliance would "go out of
area or out of business." This process continues. **[Alliance solidarity
led Europe into a protracted war in Afghanistan and the United States
into a conflagration in Libya, even though neither conflict served the
other allies' interests]{.underline}**. [Expansion grows ever more
far-fetched, with the alliance most recently adding Montenegro and North
Macedonia, small states that face no obvious threat]{.underline} and can
make no serious contribution to Europe's defense. Against significant
European opposition, moreover, the United States even supports bringing
Georgia and Ukraine into the body. As a result, some analysts today call
NATO a "global alliance," with pretensions to patrol much if not most of
the world. All the while, Washington remains responsible for the vast
majority of its combat capabilities in Europe and beyond. [The disparity
among allies' contributions causes constant friction.]{.underline} U.S.
President Barack Obama lamented Europe's "free riders." In 2011, Defense
Secretary Robert Gates warned that there would be "[dwindling appetite
and patience in the U.S. Congress---and in the American body politic
writ large---to expend increasingly precious funds on behalf of nations
that are apparently unwilling to devote the necessary resources
or]{.underline} ... to be serious and capable partners in their own
defense." Future leaders, he suggested, "[may not consider the return on
America's investment in NATO worth the cost.]{.underline}" Enter Trump.
Unfortunately, **[the burden-sharing debate has been as unproductive as
it has been lengthy.]{.underline}** Europeans expect the United States
to ride to the rescue in any crisis, so they see little reason to spend
much on their own militaries, especially during economically difficult
times at home. [Yet the same U.S. officials who complain about lagging
European defense efforts routinely reassure those allies of America's
enduring commitment.]{.underline} Washington even created the European
Reassurance Initiative, recently renamed the European Deterrence
Initiative, to increase U.S. manpower in Eastern Europe. **[Yet Europe
does not really need U.S. military support]{.underline}**. Although
scholars like the Heritage Foundation's Luke Coffey and Daniel Kochis
have argued that [Russian resurgence makes NATO as
important]{.underline} to European stability as ever, that [is
false]{.underline}. [The continent matches the U.S. economy and
possesses a larger population.]{.underline} [Meanwhile, European states
have roughly 10 times the economic strength and four times the
population of Russia. They collectively spend four times as much as
Moscow on armed forces]{.underline}. Obviously, collective action can be
difficult, and European governments need to be smarter about their
spending. However, [a sense of urgency would overcome many of these
problems.]{.underline} But that doesn't exist. Few Europeans perceive a
serious enough threat to spend more on the military. U.S. Secretary of
State Mike Pompeo acknowledged as much when he recently told a
conservative group that when he asks the Europeans what they are
prepared to do about defense, they say, "It's tough. Our voters just
really don't like to spend money on defense." Although European
countries did start to spend more after Russia intervened in Ukraine,
the uptick has barely exceeded economic growth. As a percentage of GDP,
Europe's military outlays remain below those of seven years ago. In
2014, the number, which includes Canada, fell to 1.4 percent; last year,
it was 1.47 percent. The future is not likely to be much better. To be
fair, parts of Europe, especially on NATO's edge, do appear to be more
worried about the possibility of Russian aggression. **[But even if
Russian President Vladimir Putin, despite his country's economic woes,
wanted to go after the Baltic states or Poland, he would risk further
economic isolation and almost certain military
retaliation]{.underline}**. [The Baltics' ethnic Russian minorities do
not look to Moscow as a protector and have shown little interest in
securing its rule. Poland's history promises an ever hostile population
if conquered.]{.underline} Perhaps that is why, according to Michael
Kofman, a researcher at CNA, [Russian military forces are not deployed
for combat in the Baltic region]{.underline}. "[Despite provocative air
and naval activity concentrated in the area,"]{.underline} he wrote in
2017, "[Russian forces based there are principally defensive, and aging
to boot."]{.underline} That could change, of course, but any attack
would require massive redeployments. [There would be no
cakewalk.]{.underline} [As for Russia's conflicts with Georgia and
Ukraine, they do look threatening but also appear to have an essentially
defensive purpose]{.underline}---prevent the two nations' inclusion in
NATO and protect the historic naval base of Sevastopol. Recent
diplomatic disclosures revealed that Moscow had good reason to feel
betrayed by talk of expanding the alliance. [Russia's actions cannot be
justified, of course, but if Moscow had expanded the Warsaw Pact to
Latin America, engineered a coup in Mexico City, and offered to bring
that nation into an anti-American alliance, Washington would have been
equally displeased]{.underline}. Consider Washington's sharp reaction to
Russian troops arriving in more distant Venezuela. **[Given Europe's
size, economic strength, and lack of clear and present danger, it is
time to end the fantasy of burden sharing]{.underline}**. Last year, the
United States devoted \$1,898 per person to the military. NATO's
European members spent \$503[**. If they take over the core duty of
defending Europe, they could spend what they want without hectoring from
Washington. They could conciliate or threaten Russia. They could add or
eliminate sanctions. They could make whatever decisions they wish, but
they would be responsible for the consequences.** The United States
could become an associate member of NATO, or whatever the Europeans wish
to call their new defense compact, and forge new agreements]{.underline}
with Europe to cooperate where interests coincide. Rather than remain
permanently entangled in disputes of little matter to Washington, **[it
could informally play the role of offshore balancer, prepared to respond
more directly to any unlikely hegemonic threat.]{.underline}** NATO has
reached the venerable age of 70. [It should be pensioned off and
replaced with security architecture developed to meet current
challenges]{.underline}. Instead, Pompeo quipped, member states were
meeting this week "to make sure that NATO is around for the next 70
years." [Where will Trump take NATO? He is unpredictable and inconstant.
Still, he spent years inveighing against NATO]{.underline}, and he
finally has the power to do something about it. He should insist that
Europe take over responsibility for its own security.

OUT WITH THE OLD Despite the disappointments of the past 25 years, [the

heavily skewed in favor of endless intervention.**]{.underline} Moving

parameters of debate and challenging the entrenched interests that have

reluctant to judge one another lest they be judged
themselves]{.underline}**. As a result, **[error-prone officials

mistakes.]{.underline}** [Consider the officials responsible

malfeasance. Indeed, nearly all of them still enjoy prominent positions
in government, think tanks, the media, or academia.]{.underline}** No
one is infallible, of course, and a desire to hold people accountable
could be taken too far. Policymakers often learn from past mistakes and
become more effective over time. [But when the same people keep making
the same errors and neither recognize nor regret them, it is time to

many years]{.underline}. For all his flaws, Trump has made it easier to

### AT: Parent & McDonald -- Decline Peaceful

#### **Parent & McDonald are wrong -- their study ignores regime type & generalizes poor examples**

**Schake 18** \[Kori Schake is a contributing editor at The Atlantic and
the Deputy Director-General of the International Institute for Strategic
Studies. She teaches in War Studies at King's College, "Managing
American Decline," *the Atlantic*, 11-24-2018,
<https://www.theatlantic.com/ideas/archive/2018/11/how-bad-americas-decline-relative-china/576319/>\]
sg

But this is where [I fear the aggregate data]{.underline}---prioritizing
a large number of cases all treated with equal weight---[may lead us
astray]{.underline}. Because [two things not apparent in the numbers may
prove much more important than the findings from Parent and McDonald's
study]{.underline}. **[First, it]{.underline}** probably **[matters
whether a state is declining from the top spot]{.underline}** or a spot
near the top. **[Losing the hegemon's spot matters]{.underline}** **[an
awful lot]{.underline} [more than losing an ordinal
ranking]{.underline}**[.]{.underline} There are many strong and
important countries in the world, but the [relative ranking]{.underline}
among them is of little importance. [Does Germany care whether it is the
third- or fifth-largest economy?]{.underline} Perhaps marginally, but
not fundamentally. [But countries do care whether they hold the top
spot,]{.underline} the hegemon, because that gives them the ability to
set the rules of the game. [If China becomes the hegemon, it will change
the rules]{.underline} from what they have been in the time of American
hegemony: Preference will replace law, small states will be dictated
terms by strong states---[patterns we have already begun to see in
China's intimidation of regional neighbors and predatory trade and
business practices]{.underline}. The United States and its liberal
allies may well fight to prevent those changes. **[Second, the nature of
the state matters more than Parent and McDonald credit]{.underline}**.
[They find no correlation between regime type]{.underline} (that is, the
nature of the state: communist, authoritarian, or democratic) [and war
involving a declining power]{.underline}. [But in their 16 cases of
relative decline]{.underline} since 1870, [the United Kingdom is the
declining power six times]{.underline} (Parent and McDonald elegantly
term it "the most experienced declining power"); [Germany is the rising
power eight times]{.underline}. It may be true, as Parent and McDonald
conclude, that "aggressive responses to decline appear to be the
exception, rather than the rule," but in their data, Germany is the only
state to become more aggressive and authoritarian. And for Britain to
provide so many peaceful instances of relative decline may just tell us
that Britain is exceptionally agile in playing a weakening hand well and
peacefully. **[What we]{.underline}** may be **[see]{.underline}**ing
**[in their study is less a generalizable theory of the behavior of
declining powers than a demonstration of British and German strategic
cultures]{.underline}**. They may both be anomalous, which [**makes them
poor examples on which to build a theory.** Regime type may also matter
much more because it speaks to a state's resilience.]{.underline} Parent
and McDonald find that "recovery is less likely than not, but the only
road to recovery leads through retrenchment," and that means a
government must acknowledge its circumstances and choose different
policies. [Authoritarian states tend to be more brittle than their
democratic counterparts.]{.underline} [Lacking free media]{.underline}
to publicize failures and challenge polices, [lacking distributed power
and civil society]{.underline} to experiment with alternatives and check
excess[, and lacking elections]{.underline} as competitions among
different possible directions for policy, [authoritarian governments
tend to remain committed to failing policies longer.]{.underline}
Twilight of the Titans is a meaningful contribution to the debate about
whether the decline of a great power is to be feared as a cause of war
in the international system. Parent and McDonald took a big, important
question and tried to find an answer by aggregating what we know about
both great powers and their mid-level counterparts. It is not simply an
interesting academic question; they make a very strong case that
fighting preventive wars is self-defeating for declining powers. Rather
than fight to prevent a rising challenger, states losing their relative
power should retrench and compromise to avoid conflict. Adopting Parent
and McDonald's policy recommendations, though, would be learning to live
with "democracy with Chinese characteristics." Which, interestingly
enough, is also the policy recommendation Graham Allison makes in
Destined for War.

#### INSTEX Solves

**Coppola 19** Frances Coppola is a senior contributor at Forbes
\["Europe Circumvents U.S. Sanctions On Iran," *Forbes,* 6-30-2019, URL:

[Europe has found a way of circumventing U.S. sanctions on
Iran.]{.underline} The governments of [France, Germany and the United
Kingdom have developed a special purpose vehicle]{.underline} (SPV)
[to]{.underline} enable European businesses to [maintain non-dollar
trade with Iran without breaking U.S. sanctions]{.underline}. That SPV,
[known as INSTEX]{.underline}, is now up and running. The [three
governments announced the successful implementation of INSTEX at a
meeting of the Joint Commission of the Joint Comprehensive Plan
of]{.underline} Action (JCPOA) on June 28, 2019. The meeting was chaired
on behalf of the EU by the Secretary General of the European External
Action Service (EEAS), Helga Schmid, and was attended by representatives
of China, France, Germany, Russia, the United Kingdom, and Iran. In a
statement, Schmid said: [France, Germany and the United Kingdom informed

EU Member States and that the first transactions are being
processed]{.underline}. Ongoing complementary cooperation with the

[process of joining INSTEX as shareholders, the special purpose vehicle
aimed at facilitating legitimate business with Iran]{.underline}. They
are also working to open INSTEX to economic operators from third
countries. JCPOA is better known as the "Iran nuclear deal." The U.S.
unilaterally withdrew from JCPOA in May 2018, when it reimposed
sanctions on Iran's oil export sector. [But other countries, including
EU member states, have so far declined to follow suit. They claim that
Iran is complying with the terms of the deal, and the U.S.'s decision to
reimpose sanctions was unjustified.]{.underline} When the U.S. withdrew
from JCPOA, it said that companies breaking the reimposed sanctions
would face stiff penalties. [Among the companies the U.S. administration

international messaging service SWIFT, which is the lifeblood of
